# M10 Phase 9 — Tier T1 findings — interrogation view

**Cluster:** M10 — Sin, Guilt and Transgression
**Tier:** T1
**Prompts in tier:** 24
**Characteristics:** 22
**Finding blocks below:** 528

Each prompt in this tier is shown in catalogue order. Under each prompt, all 22 characteristics' findings are stacked verbatim. No analytics, no grouping, no synthesis suggestions — just the raw data organised tier-by-tier for open exploration.

Status codes: **E** = evidenced; **S** = silent; **G** = gap.

---

## T1.1 — Name and Naming

### T1.1.1

**Question:** What is this characteristic called in the programme — and what does the name itself signal about its essential nature?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Wilful sinning." The name encodes a binary claim: (1) that sinning is a *wilful* act — not accidental, not compelled, but chosen by the inner person with awareness of what is being done; and (2) that this is *sinning* — a relational act that violates a standard. The name signals that the defining feature is not the category of wrong committed but the quality of the inner act: the will's knowing engagement with the wrong. This is confirmed by the definitional separation from CHAR-2 (Unintentional sinning) — that characteristic occupies its own sub-group precisely because the corpus distinguishes deliberate from inadvertent wrongdoing. The name "Wilful sinning" thus foregrounds volition as the constitutive element: what makes this characteristic analytically distinct is not the content of the sin but the inner quality of the will that commits it.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The programme name "Unintentional sinning" contains a qualifier ("unintentional") that is definitionally load-bearing: the name signals that the sinning act occurred without the inner person's deliberate defiance of a known command. The qualifier distinguishes this from CHAR-1 (Wilful sinning) not by minimising the moral weight of the act but by specifying the inner quality of the will at the moment of commission. Lev 4:2 uses the adverb *bishgagah* ("unintentionally / in error / by mistake") — embedded in the name is the Hebrew concept of *shegagah* (inadvertence, mistake, error), which carries the sense of wandering from the path without intending to. The name thus signals: this is real sinning (the same cha.ta term applies), but the inner-being state at the moment of the act lacked the knowing quality that characterises CHAR-1.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Confession." The name signals three things. (1) *Speech-act*: confession is inherently verbal — it is a naming, a speaking, an act that moves from inner to outer through language. (2) *Past-reference*: confession names what has *already been done* — it is retrospective, not prospective. Every instance in the corpus names a committed sin: "this time I have sinned" (Exo 9:27), "I have sinned greatly in what I have done" (2Sa 24:10), "I have sinned by betraying innocent blood" (Mat 27:4). (3) *Relational direction*: confession is always addressed to someone — God (Luk 15:18 — "against heaven and before you"), persons (Exo 10:16 — "against the Lord your God, and against you"), or a public audience (Job 33:27 — "he sings before men"). The name "Confession" thus encodes: a speech-act directed at a relational recipient, naming a past wrong as wrong.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Conscience suppression." The name encodes a specific inner-being claim: (1) *Conscience* — the moral-witness faculty that registers sin, guilt, and moral reality — is the faculty at stake; (2) *Suppression* — this faculty is not absent by original design but is being held down, refused, or prevented from operating. The name implies an active dimension: something that could function is being prevented from functioning. This is evidenced in the spectrum of the corpus: Jer 2:35 (active verbal denial — "I have not sinned" — is active suppression), Jer 16:10 (failure to recognise — passive suppression), Ecc 8:12 (ongoing sinning without the fear of God — conscience-absent by habituation), 1Cor 15:34 (ongoing sinning linked to ignorance of God — stupor rather than alert), Tit 3:11 (self-condemned but not self-aware — implicit conscience operating under explicit suppression).

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Refusal to repent." The name encodes three claims: (1) *Refusal* — this is an active act of the will, not a passive failure. The verb "refuse" implies that turning was available and the will said no. This is confirmed throughout the corpus: "he did not depart from" (2Ki 3:3; 13:2/11; 14:24; 15:9/28), "he did not turn aside from" (2Ki 10:29), "they would not obey" (Neh 9:29), "sinning deliberately" (Heb 10:26). (2) *To repent* — the thing refused is a moral turning, a departure from the sinful course. Repentance is the available alternative that the will refuses. (3) The name implies a *known alternative*: one can only refuse what one is called to do. The definition confirms this: "turning actively withheld." The name signals: the will possesses the capacity to turn, is called to turn, and chooses not to.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Habitual defection." The name encodes two claims. (1) *Habitual*: the characteristic is not a single act but a pattern settled over time — the will's default direction has been established toward sin. This contrasts with CHAR-1 (Wilful sinning — single act) and CHAR-5 (Refusal to repent — the decision-point of non-departure). The habit dimension signals that the sinning has moved beyond the episodic into the dispositional: it is the will's natural default rather than a departure from it. (2) *Defection*: the sinning is a going-away-from, an abandonment of loyalty — the image of someone who was in or should be in a covenant relationship but has repeatedly moved away from it. 2Ki 17:7 — "sinned against the Lord their God, who had brought them up out of the land of Egypt" — the redemptive background makes the sinning a defection: they were brought out, they have defected back. The name signals: a formed inner orientation of departure from God, not merely occasional sin but the will's settled direction.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Contagious sin." The name signals three things. (1) *Contagious*: the characteristic is not self-contained in the leader but spreads outward — as a disease passes from one person to another, the leader's sinful inner commitment is transmitted to the community. The contagion metaphor names the mechanism: proximity to the source (the leader) and the medium (authority relationship) combine to transmit the moral direction. (2) *Sin*: the transmitted content is specifically the leader's sinful direction, not merely their moral influence. What spreads is the wrong thing — the defection from God. (3) The name implies a *source and a recipient*: unlike other sinning characteristics that name the internal state of a single person, contagious sin requires a transmission structure — a leader (source) and a community (recipient). The name thus encodes the relational-authority framework within which the characteristic uniquely operates.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Political revolt." The name encodes three things. (1) *Political*: the characteristic operates in the political-social domain — the relationships of governing authority between peoples, nations, and kingdoms. This distinguishes it from other pa.sha registers that address theological-covenantal revolt (CHAR-19). The corpus is uniformly political: Moab against Israel (2Ki 1:1; 3:5/7), Edom and Libnah against Judah (2Ki 8:20/22; 2Ch 21:8/10), Israel against the house of David (1Ki 12:19; 2Ch 10:19). (2) *Revolt*: the characteristic is an active breaking-away — not passive non-compliance but a decisive act of defection from the governing relationship. 2Ki 8:20 — "Edom revolted from the rule of Judah and *set up a king of their own*" — the positive act of establishing independent authority is part of the revolt. (3) The name implies both a *prior relationship* (submission to authority) and a *decisive rupture* — revolt can only occur where prior submission existed.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Sinful speech." The name encodes three things. (1) *Sinful*: the speech originates in and expresses inner moral corruption — it is not merely offensive or socially inappropriate but morally sinful, carrying the weight of transgression. (2) *Speech*: the characteristic is constitutively the speech-act — the externalisation of the inner corruption through the mouth. Mat 15:19 — "out of the heart come evil thoughts... slander" — the inner (heart) and the outer (speech) are related as source and expression. (3) The name signals that the characteristic is defined by both its inner origin (sinful) and its outer form (speech) — distinguishing it from inner sinful states that remain unexpressed. The characteristic is the completion of the inner-to-outer movement: the corrupted heart speaks.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Specialised sinful mechanisms." The name signals three things. (1) *Specialised*: the mechanisms are not the general categories of sinning (wilful, unintentional, habitual) but specific named inner processes — enticement through desire, identity-falsification through collective hypocrisy, covenantal-identity abandonment through apostasy. Each mechanism names a distinct pathway through which sin captures the person. (2) *Sinful*: despite the diversity of mechanisms, all three result in moral failure — they are all pathways to sin, not merely morally neutral processes. (3) *Mechanisms*: the term signals that the characteristic is about the *how* of sinning — the inner operational process — rather than the *what* (the specific sinful act) or the *who* (the sinning person's character). CHAR-10 is the characteristic of sinning's methods.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Sin as universal condition." Three signals: (1) *Sin*: the moral-spiritual reality that separates the creature from God — not a single act but the comprehensive inner state. (2) *Universal*: the scope is every human being without exception — Rom 3:9 — "all, both Jews and Greeks, are under sin" — and Rom 3:23 — "all have sinned" — and Heb 5:3 — even the high priest must sacrifice for his own sins. The universality distinguishes this characteristic from sin-as-specific-act (CHAR-1) or sin-as-habitual-pattern (CHAR-6): CHAR-11 is the bedrock condition underlying all other sinning characteristics. (3) *Condition*: the characteristic is a structural state rather than a discrete event — "under sin" (Rom 3:9), "dead in trespasses and sins" (Eph 2:1), "sold under sin" (Rom 7:14), "die in your sin" (Joh 8:21). The name signals: every person stands in this state regardless of whether they have committed a specific sinful act, because the condition is ontological — part of their fallen human constitution.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Sin as enslaving power." Three signals. (1) *Sin*: the moral-spiritual reality at stake is sin in its power-register — not a specific act but sin operating as a force. (2) *Enslaving*: the characteristic's defining mark is that sin is not merely present but actively captures and holds the will; Joh 8:34 — "everyone who practices sin is a slave to sin" — slavery names the constitutive inner relationship. The enslaving signal distinguishes CHAR-12 from CHAR-11 (universal condition — under sin) by naming sin's *active governing function over the will* specifically. (3) *Power*: the term signals that sin operates as an active agent, not merely a state; Gal 3:22 — "the Scripture imprisoned everything under sin" — sin is the imprisoning force; it has the power to confine. The name thus encodes: sin operating as an active governing master that holds the will captive.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Sin as divine record." Three signals. (1) *Sin*: the moral reality being tracked is sin — not a neutral human limitation but moral offense against God. (2) *Divine record*: the characteristic is not the subjective experience of guilt (which would be CHAR-17) but the objective moral weight existing before God — whether the person feels it or not. The record exists independently of the person's awareness. (3) *Record*: the metaphor signals documentary retention — Hos 13:12 — "his sin is kept in store"; Exo 32:33 — "I will blot out of my book"; Jer 17:1 — "written with a pen of iron... engraved on the tablet of their heart." The name thus signals: an objective, retained, divine-witnessed moral account that is not destroyed by the person's ignorance, self-deception, or concealment from others.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Forgiveness sought and received." Three signals. (1) *Forgiveness*: the moral-relational transaction at stake — not merely guilt-relief but the objective removal of the sin-record that separates the person from God; (2) *Sought*: the inner movement originates in the person — a volitional turning toward God to appeal for what only God can grant; Act 2:38 — "repent and be baptized... for the forgiveness of your sins" — the seeking is a commanded human movement; (3) *Received*: the characteristic names completion — not merely seeking but the divine response of granting; Luk 7:48 — "your sins are forgiven" — the declaration closes the transaction. The name's compound form ("sought and received") signals the characteristic's bi-directional structure: the human movement of seeking and the divine act of granting are both necessary constituents.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Generational sin." Two signals. (1) *Generational*: the defining mark is the transmission-across-generations dimension — the sin-condition is not merely the individual's own accumulated acts but enters through lineage, family, or national belonging; Isa 43:27 — "your first father sinned" — the present generation is addressed through the ancestral chain. (2) *Sin*: the transmitted condition is specifically a moral condition — guilt or moral liability, not merely a cultural or behavioural tendency. The name signals: a sin-condition whose origin and weight extends beyond the individual to encompass ancestors and the communities that shaped them.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The programme names this characteristic "The sinner as moral character." Three signals. (1) *The sinner*: the noun-form — not "sinning" (the act) or "sinfulness" (the condition) but "the sinner" as a person-type; the definite article signals a recognisable category. (2) *As moral character*: the characteristic is a constituted inner moral identity — who the person *is* at their moral core, not what they have done or stand in. (3) *Moral*: the characterisation is specifically moral, not social or cultural — though the social dimension (VCG-01's exclusion and stigma) is a consequence. The name signals: sin has become a defining feature of the person's moral identity, not merely their behaviour or legal standing.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Guilt as inner-being state." Three signals. (1) *Guilt*: the moral condition of having violated a standard and bearing its weight — not merely the legal verdict but the lived inner experience of that verdict; (2) *As inner-being state*: the characteristic is specifically the subjective inner experience, not the objective record (CHAR-13 — sin as divine record) or the act (CHAR-1). The "state" naming signals duration: guilt is not a momentary sensation but a condition the person inhabits; (3) *Inner-being*: the guilt operates within — it is the conscience's recognition of moral liability before God. The name signals: a sustained inner-experiential condition of moral-liability awareness, distinguishable from the act that produced it and the divine record that holds it.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Iniquity as accumulated moral crime." Three signals. (1) *Iniquity*: the English rendering of a.von (H5771G) — the OT's comprehensive term for moral wrongdoing at its deepest register; not merely a sinning act but the moral weight of that act accumulated before God; (2) *As accumulated*: the defining modifier — iniquity here is not the single act but the store of wrongdoing built up over time and generations; Gen 15:16 — "not yet complete" — the accumulation is quantifiable and progressive; (3) *Moral crime*: the "crime" dimension signals the forensic register — iniquity-as-crime stands before a judge (God) as an offence requiring adjudication. The name signals: the person's moral account of wrongdoing before God, built up over time, constituting a standing moral-legal liability.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing." Three signals. (1) *Transgression*: the English rendering of the primary vocabulary family (pe.sha/pa.sha; parabasis); the word carries both the legal sense of crossing a line and the relational sense of betrayal; (2) *As deliberate*: the defining modifier — not inadvertent error (CHAR-2) but knowing, wilful choice; Rom 4:15 — "where there is no law there is no transgression" — a known boundary is required; (3) *Boundary-crossing*: the spatial-moral image that names the core act — the will steps over a line it knows to exist. The name signals: the most distinctively volitional category in M10 — the will that knows and chooses to defy.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin." Three signals. (1) *Faithlessness*: not the discrete act of sin but the dispositional inner quality of the person who breaks covenant — the opposite of the faithfulness (*ʾĕmûnāh*) that the image-bearer was designed to sustain; (2) *As covenant-breaking*: the defining context — faithlessness is not generic disloyalty but specifically the breaking of a known covenantal bond; Mal 2:14 — "your wife by covenant" — the covenant is what makes the faithlessness betrayal rather than mere preference-change; (3) *Sin*: the moral-theological classification — faithlessness is not merely relational failure but sin against God and the covenant-order. The name signals: the most relational sin-category in M10 — the will that dissolves what God constituted to be binding.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Perversion as inner inversion." Three signals. (1) *Perversion*: the English rendering covers moral distortion, twisting, and corruption — the turning of what was designed to be straight into something bent; (2) *As inner inversion*: the defining modifier — the perversity operates from within, not merely as external misbehaviour; the inversion is of the moral order from inside the person; (3) *Inner*: the characteristic is at the level of the person's moral faculties — mind, heart, will, speech — not merely behavioural. The name signals: the most comprehensive inner distortion-category in M10 — the person whose moral-inner constitution has been twisted against its grain.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The programme names this characteristic "Injustice as moral failure of right conduct." Three signals. (1) *Injustice*: the antonym of justice (*mishpat*/dikaiosynē) — what violates the right standard in dealings with persons; (2) *As moral failure*: not merely legal breach but the inner-moral dimension — the heart's orientation toward wrong conduct against others; (3) *Of right conduct*: the failure is specifically of right-conduct (*tsedaqah*/*dikaiosynē*) — the M26 cross-register cluster. The name signals: M10's most explicitly other-directed characteristic — injustice is done *toward* persons and *against* right order; it cannot exist without a victim and a violated standard.

---

### T1.1.2

**Question:** What do the primary Hebrew and Greek terms reveal at the definitional level — before deeper lexical analysis begins?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The primary Hebrew term is cha.ta (H2398), present in all 86 OT verses of the corpus. At the definitional level, cha.ta is a verb — an action-term — meaning to miss the mark, to offend, to incur guilt. The NT primary term is hamartanō (G0264), present in the NT VCG-05 corpus and also the interpersonal VCG-02 instances (Mat 18:15, Luk 17:3/4). Hamartanō is likewise a verb, a direct Greek counterpart to cha.ta in semantic range. The paraptōma (G3900) appears once in VCG-05 (Gal 6:1 — "caught in any transgression"). The definitional-level reading of both primary terms reveals that sinning is expressed verbally — as an event, an act, a thing done or committed — rather than as a static condition or enduring state. Both terms foreground the act of sinning over the accumulated condition of sin.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The primary Hebrew term is **cha.ta (H2398)** accompanied by **bishgagah** (the adverbial qualifier, "unintentionally / in error") — the combination names the act (cha.ta = sin, miss the mark) and its volitional quality (bishgagah = without conscious defiance). Lev 4:2 and Num 15:27/28 both use this construction: *ki yecheta bishgagah* — "when he sins in error." The VCG-02 verses use cha.ta in the Piel stem as a denominative verb meaning "to de-sin / purify" (the altar, sanctuary) — a distinct usage that does not name a personal moral act at all but the ritual removal of impurity from a sacred object. At the definitional level, the moral-unintentional register (VCG-01) involves a person's act; the cultic-purification register (VCG-02) involves a process applied to a place or object.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The primary Hebrew term is **cha.ta (H2398)** in its perfect-tense form: "cha.ta.ti" — "I have sinned." This is the same root as CHAR-1 and CHAR-2, but in CHAR-3 the characteristic is not the sinning itself but the *naming of the sinning* — the confessional form. The definitive grammatical marker is the first-person perfect (or its corporate equivalent "sinned we/our fathers"): the act is complete, and the confession names it as such. The NT primary term is **hamartanō (G0264)** in the aorist/perfect confession form: Mat 27:4 ("I have sinned"), Luk 15:18/21 ("I have sinned against heaven and before you"). At the definitional level, both terms reveal that confession deploys the sinning-vocabulary in the self-application mode — not describing someone else's sin but naming one's own.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The primary term is **cha.ta (H2398)** and its participial/person-type forms, used here not in the confessional mode (CHAR-3) but in the *unacknowledged* mode — sinning that is not named as such by the sinner. Jer 2:35 — "I have not sinned" (*lo chatati*) — the negative first-person perfect is the definitive evidence: the sinning vocabulary is explicitly denied. Jer 16:10 — "what is the sin that we have committed?" — the question-form of *chatanu* (we sinned) applied without self-recognition. The NT terms are **hamartanō (G0264)** (1Cor 15:34 — "do not go on sinning") applied to those in a stupor, and the adjectival *hamartōlos* equivalent in Tit 3:11 ("sinful"). At the definitional level: the sinning vocabulary is present throughout, but conscience's normal response (recognition, confession) is absent. The characteristic is defined by the gap between the objective sinning fact and the person's absent or refused inner recognition.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The primary Hebrew term is **cha.ta (H2398)** in the phrase construction "did not depart from the sins of..." — the characteristic is not expressed by a single dedicated term but by the combination of cha.ta (sins) with verbs of non-departure: *lo sar min* (did not turn aside from — 2Ki 10:29), *lo sar min* (did not depart from — 2Ki 13:2/11), *lo sur* (did not turn away — 2Ki 15:24), "clung to" (*davaq* — 2Ki 3:3). The secondary OT term is in Neh 9:29 where sinning (*chatanu*) is accompanied by the physical metaphor "stubborn shoulder" (*natan kateph sorerah* — gave a stubborn shoulder) and "stiffened their neck" (*qashu et-orpam*). The primary NT term is **hamartanō (G0264)** in its persistent/deliberate mode: 1Ti 5:20 — "persist in sin" (*hamartanontōn*); Heb 10:26 — "go on sinning deliberately" (*hekousiōs hamartanontōn*). The definitional revelation: the characteristic has no single dedicated term but is expressed through the combination of sinning-vocabulary with non-departure language.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The primary Hebrew term is **cha.ta (H2398)** in its person-type participial/nominal forms: *hachotet* ("the sinner" — Ecc 7:26; 9:2/18; Pro 11:31; 13:22) and as a verb in pattern-contexts (Exo 9:34 — "sinned yet again"; Hos 8:11/10:9 — recurring sinning; Heb 3:17). The participial/nominal form *hachotet* is the distinctive marker of this characteristic: it names the settled person-type ("the sinner") rather than a specific act of sinning. The NT primary term is **hamartanō (G0264)** in the *present continuous* form: 1Jo 3:6 — *hamartanōn* (present participle: "the one keeping on sinning") — the continuous present aspect names the ongoing pattern rather than the completed act. The definitional revelation: habitual defection is expressed primarily through *person-type* terms (the sinner) and *continuous-action* forms (keeps on sinning), both of which distinguish settled pattern from single act.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The defining Hebrew term is the **Hiphil stem of cha.ta (H2398)**: *heheti* — "caused to sin," "made to sin." This is the causative form of the standard sinning verb: where the Qal (basic) stem names the person who sins themselves (CHAR-1/2/4/5/6), the Hiphil names the person who causes or enables another's sinning. The Hiphil construction appears across the corpus in the formula *heheti et-Israel* ("he made Israel to sin") and its Judah equivalent. This grammatical distinction — Qal personal sinning vs Hiphil caused-sinning — is the vocabulary-level marker that separates CHAR-7 from all adjacent characteristics. The NT does not contribute distinct terms in the 19-verse corpus; the characteristic is an OT-Hebrew phenomenon expressed through the Hiphil causative. The definitional revelation: CHAR-7 is the characteristic that the Hiphil of cha.ta uniquely names — sinning that operates through causing another to sin.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The primary term is **pa.sha (H6586)** in its political-revolt register. The root meaning of pa.sha is *to break away from, to revolt, to transgress* — it names the action of decisive separation from an established binding relationship. In CHAR-8's political register, pa.sha is used exclusively of the breaking of governing authority relationships: "Moab rebelled against Israel" (2Ki 1:1), "Edom revolted from the rule of Judah" (2Ki 8:20), "Israel has been in rebellion against the house of David" (1Ki 12:19). At the definitional level, pa.sha reveals that political revolt is not merely passive non-compliance but an active transgressive breaking-away — the same root that names sin-as-transgression in CHAR-19 is here applied to the political-authority domain, confirming that political revolt carries the full moral weight of wilful transgression. No NT Greek vocabulary appears in the 9-verse corpus — the characteristic is exclusively OT Hebrew.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The primary term is **blasfēmia (G0988)** — noun — and **blasfēmos (G0989)** — adjective. The Greek root combines *blapto* (to harm, injure) and *phēmi* (to speak) — literally "harmful speech," "injurious speech." At the definitional level, the root reveals: sinful speech is speech that harms — directed at God (blasphemy in the theological sense) or at persons (slander). The 22-verse corpus is exclusively NT Greek; no OT Hebrew vocabulary appears in CHAR-9 (the OT pa.sha/cha.ta registers for verbal sin are covered in other characteristics). The Greek blasfēmia thus encompasses both the God-directed (blasphemy-proper) and person-directed (slander) registers — its semantic range is wider than the English "blasphemy" suggests.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Three distinct Greek terms, each naming a different mechanism. (1) **deleazō (G1185)**: *to lure, to entice, to bait* — from *delear* (bait). The fishing/trapping metaphor is embedded in the root: enticement is the placing of bait before the creature that draws it toward capture. Jam 1:14 — "lured and enticed" — uses the term in the passive: the person is the one being drawn by their own desire. (2) **sunupokrinomai (G4942)**: *sun-* (together, with) + *hupokrinomai* (to act, to play a part, to pretend) — literally "to act hypocritically together/alongside." The compound names collective complicity in identity-performance. (3) **apostasia (G0646)**: *apo-* (away from) + *stasis* (standing) — "a standing away from," a departure, a defection. The root reveals: apostasia is a positional term — the person was standing in one place (the covenant) and has moved away from it.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Three term-groups across the corpus. (1) *OT Hebrew terms (VCG-01)*: **rish.ah (H7564)** — wickedness, moral-evil as a defining quality (Isa 9:18; Mal 1:4; 3:15; 4:1) — the root names the condition of being wicked as a settled identity; **al.vah (H5932)** — iniquity as a defining moral characterisation (Hos 10:9) — the unjust as a constituted type. (2) *NT noun*: **hamartia (G0266)** — the dominant term (majority of the 112 verses) — miss the mark, fall short. The noun form (as distinct from the verb) is what VCG-03 deploys to name sin as a power: "sin reigned" (Rom 5:21), "under sin" (Rom 3:9), "sin that dwells within me" (Rom 7:17). The noun form names the condition rather than the act. (3) *NT noun*: **paraptōma (G3900)** — trespass, false step, falling aside (Rom 4:25; 5:16; 11:11/12; Eph 2:1/2:5) — specifically the relational-fall dimension, naming sin as an offense/stumble rather than just a condition. (4) *NT verb*: **hamartanō (G0264)** — to sin (Rom 2:12; 3:23; 5:12/14/16; 1Cor 7:28/36) — used in the universal-condition context to name the completed-act dimension of the universal state.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The primary NT Greek terms are: (1) **hamartia (G0266)** — noun — deployed in the enslaving-power register: Joh 8:34 — "slave to sin" (hamartias — genitive, naming sin as the master); Rom 6:1/2 — "continue in sin... live in it" (singular noun — sin as domain); Gal 3:22 — "imprisoned everything under sin" (hamartian — accusative, sin as the imprisoning power). The noun form names sin as an entity with governing authority. (2) **hamartanō (G0264)** — verb — in the practice/habit register: Eph 4:26 — "do not sin"; 1Jo 3:8/9 — "makes a practice of sinning"; 1Jo 5:16/18 — "sinning... does not keep on sinning." The verb's present continuous form in 1Jo 3:8/9/5:18 — the present participle *hamartanōn* — names habitual ongoing sinning as the condition of enslavement. No OT Hebrew vocabulary appears; the characteristic is exclusively NT Greek.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Five term-groups across the 205 verses. (1) **chat.tat (H2403B)** — the dominant term (VCG-05 — 113 verses): sin as moral fact, wrong committed, the act that enters the record. The noun form names the sin-entity as a reality requiring priestly atonement (Lev 4:23/26/28/35) or divine forgiveness. (2) **cha.ta.ah (H2401/H2403A)** — sin as objective moral category requiring covering (VCG-02 — 9 verses): Psa 32:1 — "whose sin is covered"; Jer 17:1 — "sin of Judah is written... engraved on the tablet of their heart." (3) **chet (H2399)** — personal moral liability (VCG-01 — 32 verses): Num 9:13 — "that man shall bear his sin"; Deu 24:16 — "each one shall be put to death for his own sin." The bearer-of-sin framing is distinctive: chet names the personal carrying of moral liability. (4) **cha.ta (H2398)** — the verb (numerous VCG-05 verses): "whoever has sinned against me" (Exo 32:33) — the act of sinning that creates the record entry. (5) **hamartia/hamartēma (G0266/G0265)** — NT terms: Joh 1:29 — "the sin of the world" as a collective burden lifted by the Lamb; Rom 3:25 — "former sins" passed over in divine forbearance.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Four term-groups. (1) **kip.pu.rim (H3725)** (VCG-01 — 12 verses): the noun-plural of kāpar — "coverings/atonements." The root kāpar means "to cover, to atone, to make expiation" — the mechanism is concealment/covering of the sin-record. The plural form names not a single act but the institution: the Day of Atonement as a structured series of covering acts. (2) **chat.tat (H2403B/H2403H)** (VCG-01/03): the sin-offering as the ritual vehicle of seeking forgiveness — the animal bears the sin that the person brings. (3) **cha.ta.ah (H2401)** (VCG-02): the sin named before God in the seeking-context (Exo 32:30/31 — Moses naming the great sin). (4) **hamartia (G0266) / hamartēma (G0265) / paraptōma (G3900)** (VCG-02): NT terms for the sin/trespasses whose forgiveness is declared through Christ's blood (Mat 26:28; Mar 3:28/29; Eph 1:7; Col 2:13).

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The single primary term across all 8 verses is **cha.ta (H2398)** — the verb "to sin, to miss the mark, to incur guilt." The verb form throughout this corpus is significant: generational sin is evidenced through the *act of sinning* as an inherited pattern, not through a distinct noun for the inherited condition itself. Isa 43:27 — "your first father sinned" (*cha.ta*) — the past-tense verb names the ancestral sinning act that initiated the generational chain; Psa 78:17 — "they sinned still more" — the repeated sinning reinforces the pattern. No specialised vocabulary for the inherited-condition dimension exists in this 8-verse corpus; the inherited condition is described through the repeated sinning-verb applied to successive generations.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Two term-groups. (1) **chat.ta (H2400)** — noun form (OT — 14 verses in VCG-03): the agent-noun "one who sins habitually, a sinful person." The noun form distinguishes from the verb (cha.ta — the act): chat.ta names the person constituted by sinning rather than the sinning act. Gen 13:13 — "great sinners" (*chat.ta.im gĕdōlîm*) — the plural noun names a category of persons; 1Sa 15:18 — "the sinners, the Amalekites" — chat.ta is used as an identity descriptor applied to a people. (2) **hamartōlos (G0268)** — noun/adjective (NT — 50 verses across all VCGs): "sinful person, sinner." The Greek term functions both as a noun (the sinner — Luk 7:37) and as an adjective (sinful — Luk 5:8 — "I am a sinful man"; Mar 8:38 — "sinful generation"). Both terms are agent-nouns naming the person constituted by sin rather than the act itself.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Four Hebrew term-groups across the 97 verses. (1) **a.sham (H0816)** — verb "to be guilty, to incur guilt, to realise one's guilt" (VCG-01 dominant): Lev 4:13/22/27; 5:2/3/4/5/17/19 — the guilt-realisation sequence; the verb form emphasises the moment of coming-to-awareness. (2) **a.shem (H0818)** — noun form "a guilty one, guilt" (VCG-01): Gen 42:21 — "in truth we are guilty"; Ezr 10:19 — "their guilt offering was a ram." (3) **ash.mah (H0819)** — noun "guilt, guiltiness" (VCG-02 dominant): Ezr 9:6 — "our guilt has mounted up to the heavens"; the noun names the accumulated condition. (4) **a.von (H5771H/I)** — "iniquity, guilt, punishment" (VCG-03/04): H5771H names the guilt as transferable burden (Gen 44:16; Exo 28:38); H5771I names the punitive consequence (Gen 4:13 — "my punishment"; Eze 4:4/5/6). (5) **pu.qah (H6330)** — "stumbling, staggering of conscience" (1Sa 25:31 — single verse, M03 cross-register): names the destabilising inner effect of guilt-awareness on the conscience.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Single primary term throughout the entire 162-verse corpus: **a.von (H5771G)** — the crime/moral-wrong sub-entry of the a.von root. The root (*ʿāwôn*) spans three distinct senses within M10's framework: H5771G (crime/moral-wrong — CHAR-18), H5771H (guilt/liability — CHAR-17 VCG-03), and H5771I (punishment/consequence — CHAR-17 VCG-04). The H5771G crime-sense names the wrongdoing itself as a moral reality before God — what Job calls "my iniquity" in the sense of "my moral crime" (Job 7:21 — "take away my iniquity"; Job 13:23 — "how many are my iniquities?"). No NT Greek vocabulary appears in this corpus; the characteristic is exclusively OT Hebrew, reflecting the a.von root's primary OT-framework operation.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Four primary term-groups. (1) **pe.sha (H6588)** — noun "transgression, rebellion, breach" — OT dominant (most VCGs); root sense of breaking away from, revolting against; (2) **pa.sha (H6586)** — verb "to transgress, to rebel, to break away" — action-form of the same root; Eze 2:3 — "nations of rebels who have rebelled (*pa.sha*) against me"; (3) **sa.rah (H5627)** — noun "revolt, apostasy, turning aside" — VCG-02; Isa 1:5 — continued revolt despite punishment; Jer 28:16 — "spoken rebellion against the Lord"; (4) **parabasis (G3847) / parabatēs (G3848) / parabainō (G3845)** — NT "transgression/transgressor/to transgress" — VCG-04; Rom 4:15 — definitional: "where there is no law there is no transgression"; (5) **paraptōma (G3900)** — "trespass, false step" — VCG-05 — Adam's paradigm (Rom 5:15/16/17/18/20). The term-families converge on the same semantic core: the will choosing to step past a known boundary.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Two primary term-families, both exclusively OT Hebrew with no NT vocabulary. (1) **ma.al (H4603/H4604)** — verb/noun "to commit a breach of faith, to act unfaithfully, to be treacherous" — VCG-01 dominant (unfaithfulness toward God); the root implies a violation of sacred trust, a transgression of what is consecrated or owed; Lev 5:15 — "breach of faith" in holy things; 1Ch 10:13 — "breach of faith with the Lord"; (2) **ba.gad (H0898)** — verb "to act treacherously, to be faithless, to betray" — VCG-02/03 dominant (interpersonal and constitutional treachery); the root names the inner disposition of betrayal; Mal 2:14 — "been faithless" (*bāgadtāh*) to the wife of one's youth; Isa 48:8 — "deal treacherously" as a foreknown constitutional bent. Both terms name the relational-covenantal dimension of the betrayal as the defining feature.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Nine distinct term-families, the highest of any M10 characteristic. (1) **tah.pu.khah (H8419)** — "perverseness, frowardness" (VCG-01/03 dominant): the root *hāpak* means "to turn, to overturn" — what has been overturned; (2) **la.zut (H3891)** — "perversity, crookedness" (VCG-01 — Pro 4:24); (3) **sa.laph (H5557)** — "to subvert, to overthrow, to pervert" (VCG-02 — judgment perversion); (4) **a.vah (H5753A/B)** — "to be crooked, to twist, to do iniquity" (VCG-03 dominant); (5) **te.vel (H8397)** — "perversion, violation of natural/created order" (VCG-04 — Lev 18:23/20:12); (6) **sur (H5494)** — "to turn aside, to degenerate" (VCG-04 — Jer 2:21/17:13); (7) **cha.val (H2254B)** — "to destroy, to corrupt, to ruin" (VCG-05 OT — M03 cross-register); (8) **nav.lut (H5040)** — "lewdness, shame" (VCG-05 — Hos 2:10 — M07 cross-register); (9) **ftheirō/fthora/diaftheirō (G5351/5356/1311)** — NT "to corrupt, to destroy, corruption/decay" (VCG-05 NT dominant). The terms collectively reveal: perversion as twisting of the given order (tah.pu.khah/a.vah), violation of created categories (te.vel), judgment-subversion (sa.laph), and progressive decay (ftheirō/fthora).

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Five primary term-families. (1) **a.vel / av.lah (H5766A/H5766B)** — noun/verb "injustice, wrong, wickedness, violence" — OT dominant (VCG-01/02); root *ʿāwal* — "to act unjustly, to be crooked"; (2) **av.val (H5767)** — "the unrighteous, the unjust person" — agent-noun (VCG-03 OT); (3) **a.vil (H5760)** — "ungodly, morally lawless" — VCG-03 OT (Job 16:11; Job 18:21); (4) **adikos (G0094)** — NT "unjust, unrighteous" — agent-noun/adjective (VCG-03 NT dominant); (5) **adikēma (G0092)** — NT "wrongdoing, crime, injustice" — the act-noun (Act 18:14; Rev 18:5). The terms cluster around two axes: the wrong-act dimension (a.vel/av.lah/adikēma — what is done) and the wrong-person dimension (av.val/a.vil/adikos — who the person is).

---

### T1.1.3

**Question:** Does the name carry directional, relational, or constitutional implications that orient the enquiry from the outset?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The name "Wilful sinning" carries strong relational and directional implications. The corpus structures sinning along three directional axes: (1) vertical — sinning against God (VCG-01: "sin against God" Gen 39:9; "against you, you only" Psa 51:4; "sinned against me" Eze 14:13); (2) horizontal — sinning against persons (VCG-02: "sin against his neighbor" 1Ki 8:31; "sins against you" Mat 18:15); and (3) reflexive — sinning against oneself (1Cor 6:18: "the sexually immoral person sins against his own body"; Pro 8:36: sinning injures oneself). These three directions are constitutive: the name implies that wilful sinning is always sinning *against* someone — it is irreducibly relational. The constitutional implication is that sinning engages the will in a movement *away from* a relational bond that was owed, making the will the primary constitutional faculty at stake.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The name carries primarily *constitutional* implications: unintentional sinning locates the characteristic within the inner person's epistemic constitution — the capacity to act without full knowledge. The relational implication is present but muted compared to CHAR-1: Lev 4:2 ("in any of the Lord's commandments about things not to be done") locates the standard relationally (God's commands), but the violation occurs without the relational *defiance* that characterises wilful sinning. The directional implication is toward God: even uninstructed violation is "unintentional in any of the Lord's commandments" — God remains the reference point. But the direction lacks the volitional turning-against that VCG-01 of CHAR-1 carries. Constitutional epistemic limitation is the defining implication.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: confession moves from inner (the conscientious recognition) to outer (the spoken word) — 2Sa 24:10 shows the sequence: "David's heart struck him" (inner) → "David said to the Lord" (outer direction, God-ward). *Relational*: every confession is addressed to a party: God (VCG-01 predominant), persons (Exo 10:16 — "against you"), or a public community (VCG-02 corporate confessions). The relational direction is constitutive — confession is not self-addressed monologue but a speech-act directed at a relational other. *Constitutional*: confession engages the conscience (the inner witness), the will (which must agree to name the act as wrong), and the mouth (through which the naming is enacted). The constitutional depth runs from conscience to will to speech.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The name carries primarily *constitutional* implications: the faculty of conscience is a constituted inner capacity, and suppression names what is done to that capacity. The constitutional implication is a malfunction at the conscience-level of the inner person. *Relational* implications are secondary but present: Jer 2:35 — the denial of sin is directed at God ("I am innocent; surely his anger has turned from me") — the suppression operates in the vertical relational context, claiming a standing before God that does not exist. The directional implication runs *inward*: conscience suppression prevents the inward moral recognition that should move toward confession and repentance. It is a blockage of the inward-to-outward moral movement that CHAR-3 (Confession) represents.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: the refusal to repent is a refusal to turn — the will is held in a direction away from God and away from righteousness; the turning that is refused would be a directional change. "He did not turn aside from... he did not depart from" (2Ki 10:29; 13:2) — the language is directional. *Relational*: the refusal is against God's law (2Ki 10:31 — "not careful to walk in the law of the Lord") and against his warnings (Neh 9:29 — "you warned them... yet they would not obey"). The refusal is exercised in the context of a relational claim. *Constitutional*: Neh 9:29 — "stubborn shoulder and stiffened their neck" — these bodily-metaphor terms describe a constitutional posture of resistance, not a single act. 2Ki 10:31 — "not with all his heart" — the constitutional incompleteness of the inner commitment.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: "defection" names a movement away-from — the habitual sinner is oriented away from God and toward sin as the default direction. 2Ki 17:7 — "sinned against the Lord... and had feared other gods" — the directional movement is from God-fearing to other-gods-fearing, a reorientation of the will's basic directional commitment. *Relational*: habitual defection occurs within and against a relational context — the Lord who "brought them up out of the land of Egypt" (2Ki 17:7) is the relational foundation against which the defection is measured. *Constitutional*: "habitual" signals a constitutional formation — the will has been shaped into its sinning direction by repeated acts. 1Jo 3:6's "no one who keeps on sinning has either seen him or known him" names a constitutional-relational condition: the habitual sinner lacks the fundamental inner-being relationship with Christ.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: contagious sin moves from inner (the leader's sinful commitment) to outer (the community's sinning) — the direction is source-to-recipient, inner-to-community. 2Ki 17:21 — "Jeroboam drove Israel from following the Lord" — the directional movement is explicitly outward from the leader's act to the community's redirection. *Relational*: the transmission requires the authority relationship — 1Ki 16:2 — "I made you leader over my people Israel" — the leader-people relationship is the medium through which sin travels. Without the authority relationship, there is no contagious sin, only personal sinning. *Constitutional*: the community's inner moral orientation is reshaped by the leader's transmitted sinning — what was oriented toward God is driven away (2Ki 17:21) and turned toward idols (2Ki 21:11). The recipients' constitutional inner direction is altered.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: revolt is a movement away from — breaking out of the governing relationship, moving from submission to independence. 2Ki 8:20 — "revolted *from* the rule of Judah" — the directional preposition (*min* — from) names the movement away from authority. *Relational*: revolt requires a pre-existing governing relationship; the revolt breaks that relationship. 2Ki 3:7 — "the king of Moab has rebelled against me" — the revolt is understood within a prior suzerain-vassal relationship. *Constitutional*: 2Ch 21:10's causal link — "because he had forsaken the Lord" — implies that the inner constitutional condition of forsaking God is what produces the outward political revolt. The political revolt is the constitutional expression of the inner spiritual abandonment.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: sinful speech moves from inner (the corrupted heart — Mat 15:19) to outer (the spoken word). The direction is inner-to-outer, with the speech being the externalisation of the inner condition. *Relational*: sinful speech is always directed at a relational target — God (VCG-02: Mar 2:7; Joh 10:33; VCG-03: Rev 13:6) or persons (VCG-01: Eph 4:31; Col 3:8). The characteristic has no non-relational form — it is always aimed. *Constitutional*: Mat 15:19 — the heart is the constitutional origin; the speech is its symptom. The constitutional implication is: sinful speech reveals the heart's condition. 1Ti 6:4 — conceit and diseased will as the constitutional ground.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — All three mechanisms carry distinct directional and relational implications. *Enticement (deleazō)*: directional — the lure draws the person from their current position toward sin (Jam 1:14 — "lured and enticed"); relational — the enticement operates through the person's own desire, making the relationship between the self's desire-faculty and the temptation the operative relational field. *Hypocrisy (sunupokrinomai)*: directional — the person moves from authentic identity toward performed identity; relational — the collective dimension (*sun-*) makes it constitutively social, requiring others to hypo-krinomai alongside. *Apostasy (apostasia)*: directional — a standing-away-from, a departure; relational — the departure is from a covenantal relational standing (the Mosaic covenant — Act 21:21).

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — All three implications are present at characteristic scope. *Directional*: Rom 3:23 — "fall short of the glory of God" — the direction is downward (falling short, not reaching the standard) and inward (the condition is in the person, not merely their acts). *Relational*: the condition is always measured against God — "under sin" (Rom 3:9) is a relational position relative to a power; Joh 8:21 — "die in your sin" where Jesus is the one departing — the relational consequence of dying in sin is separation from where Jesus goes. Heb 5:1 — sin creates the need for priestly mediation between human and divine — the relational distance is the characteristic's relational implication. *Constitutional*: the condition is a structural feature of every human person — Joh 9:34 — "born in utter sin" — the birth-origin framing; Eph 2:1 — "dead in trespasses and sins" — a constituted inner state, not an external label.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — All three implications are present. *Directional*: the enslaving power moves inward — from sin's external pull to the internal capture of the will; Joh 8:34's practice (outward acts) → slavery (inner constitutional captivity) reveals the direction. *Relational*: the characteristic is constitutively relational — a master-slave relationship between sin (master) and the person (slave — Joh 8:34). *Constitutional*: 1Jo 3:9 — "God's seed abides in him... he cannot keep on sinning" — the cannot-keep-sinning is a constitutional impossibility grounded in the new birth; the liberation from the enslaving power is equally constitutional. The name thus implies: the will's constitutional direction, the relational master-slave structure, and the inner-directional movement of capture.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — All three. *Directional*: the record accumulates in one direction — from the person upward toward God; Isa 59:12 — "our sins testify against us" — the testimony runs from the person toward divine judgment. *Relational*: the record is constitutively relational — it exists before God (Lev 4:26 — "the priest shall make atonement for him for his sin, and he shall be forgiven" — the atonement addresses a relational deficit). *Constitutional*: Jer 17:1 — "engraved on the tablet of their heart" — the record is also internally inscribed; Psa 51:5 — "in sin did my mother conceive me" — the record begins constitutionally, at birth. The directional, relational, and constitutional dimensions are all evidenced in the 205-verse corpus.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — All three. *Directional*: "sought" names a movement from the person toward God; "received" names the return movement from God to the person. The directional structure is toward-God then from-God — a bilateral movement that constitutes the full forgiveness-event. *Relational*: the characteristic is constitutively relational — it requires the person-God relationship as its operative field; forgiveness cannot be sought from or granted by anyone else (Mar 2:7 context — "who can forgive sins but God alone?" — referenced in CHAR-11 but structurally operative here). *Constitutional*: Act 2:38 — repentance + baptism → forgiveness → Spirit-reception — the constitutional change (receiving the Spirit) is the consequence of the characteristic's completion.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — All three. *Directional*: the condition moves backward (from ancestors to present) and forward (from present to descendants); Isa 43:27's "first father sinned" is the backward direction; the ongoing pattern (Psa 78:17/32 — still sinning) is the forward continuation. *Relational*: the condition is constitutively relational — it operates within lineages, families, and covenant communities; it cannot be inherited without the relational bond of ancestry. *Constitutional*: Isa 64:5 — "in our sins we have been a long time" — the prolonged duration suggests the condition has become constitutional, not merely episodic.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — All three. *Directional*: Jam 5:20 — "whoever brings back a sinner from his wandering" — the sinner is characterized by directional drift away from God; the direction of the sinner's moral character is away-from-God. *Relational*: Psa 1:1 — "the way of sinners" as a social-moral class whose company shapes inner orientation; Luk 15:2 — Pharisees' complaint that Jesus "receives sinners and eats with them" — the relational dimension is constitutive. *Constitutional*: Rom 5:19 — "the many were made sinners" — the constitutional imputation of the sinner-identity through Adam's disobedience names this as a constituted inner status, not merely a behavioural descriptor.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — All three. *Directional*: guilt is directed toward God — Ezr 9:15 — "we are before you in our guilt" — the guilt-state faces toward the divine judge; guilt is always guilt-before-someone. *Relational*: guilt is constitutively relational — it requires both the guilty person and the one wronged; Num 5:6 — "any of the sins that people commit by breaking faith with the Lord" — guilt arises from a broken relationship; Gen 42:21 — "we are guilty concerning our brother" — guilt arises from wrong against another person. *Constitutional*: Ezr 9:6 — guilt as a condition that has mounted up over time; Hos 10:2 — "their heart is false; now they must bear their guilt" — the guilt is a constitutional weight the false-hearted person must carry.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — All three. *Directional*: iniquity has a trajectory of accumulation — Gen 15:16 — "not yet complete" — moving toward fullness; Hos 14:1 — "you have stumbled because of your iniquity" — the iniquity operates as a directional force pulling the person away from God. *Relational*: iniquity is always iniquity-before-God — Psa 90:8 — "you have set our iniquities before you" — the relational frame is the moral account in God's presence; Isa 43:24 — "you have wearied me with your iniquities" — iniquity is a relational burden between the person and God. *Constitutional*: Psa 51:5 — "I was brought forth in iniquity" — the iniquity-condition is constitutional, prior to individual acts; Eze 18:20 — "the wickedness of the wicked shall be upon himself" — it attaches constitutionally to the person who commits it.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — All three. *Directional*: transgression is movement across a boundary — the will moves from the permitted side to the forbidden side; Isa 59:20 — "those who turn from transgression" — the turn-from presupposes a direction-toward; Isa 31:6 — "turn to him from whom people have deeply revolted" — the revolt is a directional departure. *Relational*: transgression is always against someone — Jer 2:29 — "you have all transgressed against me"; Eze 2:3 — "rebelled against me"; Gen 31:36 — "what is my offense against you?" — the relational frame is constitutive. *Constitutional*: Isa 48:8 — "from before birth you were called a rebel" — transgression as a pre-personal constitutional label; Isa 57:4 — "children of transgression" — a hereditary identity.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — All three. *Directional*: faithlessness is directed against someone — Lev 26:40 — "treachery that they committed against me"; Jer 3:20 — "treacherous to me, O house of Israel" — the direction is always against a covenant-partner; *Relational*: the characteristic is constitutively relational — Mal 2:14 — "though she is your companion and your wife by covenant" — the prior covenant-bond is what makes the faithlessness treachery rather than mere departure; *Constitutional*: Isa 48:8 — "from before birth you were called a rebel... I knew you would surely deal treacherously" — faithlessness is a constitutional inner bent that God foreknew; 2Ch 28:22 — "in time of his distress he became yet more faithless" — deepening under pressure confirms constitutional character.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — All three. *Directional*: perversion always inverts something — a direction of moral movement that turns back on itself; Jer 3:21 — "perverted their way" — the directional turning of the life-path away from God; *Relational*: perversion damages the God-relationship and human relationships — Isa 29:16 — the creature denying the creator (relational inversion); Pro 6:14 — "continually sowing discord" — perversity fractures relational bonds; *Constitutional*: Eph 4:22 — "old self corrupt through deceitful desires" — the corruption is constitutional to the fallen "old self"; 1Ti 6:5 — "depraved in mind" — the depravity is at the constitutional level of the mind's functioning.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — All three. *Directional*: injustice is always directed toward someone — Psa 58:2 — "your hands deal out violence on earth" — the violence is toward others; Eze 3:20 — "commits injustice" as an act against the righteousness-standard; *Relational*: injustice damages persons — Psa 43:1 — "from the deceitful and unjust man deliver me"; Job 6:29 — "let no injustice be done" to Job; *Constitutional*: Luk 16:10 — "one who is dishonest in a very little is also dishonest in much" — injustice is a settled character trait scaling across all contexts; Zep 3:5 — "the unjust knows no shame" — the constitutional insensibility to their own wrongdoing.

---

## T1.2 — Kind

### T1.2.1

**Question:** What kind of inner-being phenomenon does this characteristic appear to be — an act, a disposition, a condition, a quality, or something else?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The verse corpus establishes wilful sinning primarily as an *act* — a discrete event of the will. The dominant grammatical form throughout is the cha.ta verb in the perfect or imperfect (committed, commits, sinned), naming an act rather than a state. "The soul who sins shall die" (Eze 18:4) and "Israel has sinned" (Jos 7:11) both describe a completed act with consequences. The cultic-atonement corpus (VCG-04) further confirms this: "the sin which he has committed" (Lev 4:28) triggers a prescribed response — an act-to-remedy structure that makes sense only if sinning is an event rather than a standing condition. However, the corpus also contains dispositional evidence: Hos 4:7 ("the more they increased, the more they sinned against me") and Hos 13:2 ("they sin more and more") describe sinning as an escalating pattern that moves beyond single acts toward an entrenched orientation. The characteristic is primarily an act but contains a dispositional trajectory when sustained.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Unintentional sinning is primarily an *act* — a specific deed committed — but one whose inner-being content is distinguished by what is absent at the moment of commission: deliberate knowledge that the act is wrong. Lev 4:2 ("if anyone sins unintentionally... and does any one of them") is act-language: the person did something. Num 15:27 ("if one person sins unintentionally") confirms the act-character. The inner-being dimension that makes it distinctive is not the act itself but the epistemological condition under which it was committed: the conscience was not engaged at the point of action because the moral reality of the act had not yet surfaced to awareness. The act-character is primary; the inner-epistemic condition is what distinguishes it from CHAR-1.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Confession is a *speech-act that enacts an inner orientation* — it is simultaneously an outer verbal event and an inner moral event. The verbal act (speaking "I have sinned") is not confession without the inner movement of conscience and will that it expresses; the inner movement is not complete confession without the verbal enactment. This dual nature is evidenced in the contrast between genuine and hollow confession: Deu 1:41 (VCG-03) — the words "we have sinned" are spoken, but the will immediately proceeds on its own — showing that the verbal act without inner transformation is definitionally hollow. Conversely, 2Sa 24:10 shows the inner conscience-movement ("heart struck him") as the primary event that the verbal confession ("I have sinned greatly") completes. Confession is thus a *conscience-to-speech event* — an inner recognition externalised through deliberate verbal naming.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The corpus reveals conscience suppression as operating across a spectrum of three kinds. (1) *A condition* (settled inner state): Ecc 8:12 — the sinner "does evil a hundred times" while the fear of God is absent — this is a habituated settled condition, not a single act. Tit 3:11 — "warped and sinful" — a constituted inner character deformation. (2) *An active denial* (speech-act): Jer 2:35 — "I have not sinned" — an explicitly voiced claim; this is more act-like (the person actively asserts innocence). (3) *An absence/blindness* (epistemic gap): Jer 16:10 — the people genuinely do not recognise their sin — this is closer to an epistemic condition (absence of moral self-knowledge) than a deliberate act. The characteristic is thus not one kind of inner-being phenomenon but three related expressions: an active denial, a habituated condition, and an epistemic blindness — all sharing the common feature of the conscience not functioning in its proper registering mode.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent is primarily a *sustained volitional disposition* — not a single act but a settled direction of the will maintained over time. The corpus's dominant form — the Kings formula repeated across 10+ kings ("did not depart from the sins of Jeroboam") — presents refusal to repent as a characteristic persisted in across reigns, even lifetimes: 2Ki 15:18 — "he did not depart all his *days* from all the sins of Jeroboam" — a lifetime's direction named explicitly. 1Ti 5:20 — "those who *persist* in sin" — the present participle naming ongoing dispositional stance. The characteristic is not one act of not turning but the settled dispositional commitment to remain on the sinful course.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection is a *settled dispositional condition* — a formed pattern rather than an act or episode. The dominant evidence shape in the corpus is person-type language: *hachotet* (the sinner — Ecc 7:26; 9:2/18; Pro 11:31/13:22) names a constituted inner orientation rather than an event. 1Jo 3:6's "keeps on sinning" (*hamartanōn* — present continuous) is the verbal equivalent of the person-type: it names a continuous state of the will rather than a discrete choice. The single act-evidence (Exo 9:34 — "sinned yet again") becomes habitual only through the "yet again" — it is the repetition that makes it habitual. The characteristic is dispositional: it names the will's settled direction, the inner orientation that produces sinning as the default output rather than the exception.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin is a *relational-causal inner-being phenomenon* — it requires two inner-being realities in a causal relationship. (1) The *leader's inner commitment to the sinful course*: 1Ki 16:19 — "walking in the way of Jeroboam" — the leader's personal inner direction is the source. The sinning is not accidental but reflects the leader's own settled will. (2) The *community's redirected inner orientation*: 2Ki 17:21 — Jeroboam "made them commit great sin" — the community's inner direction has been redirected by the leader's influence. The characteristic is not purely an inner-being state (it requires two parties) nor purely a social phenomenon (it originates in and produces inner-being states in both parties). It is uniquely a *transmitted inner moral direction* — the leader's inner sinful orientation flowing through authority to reshape the community's inner orientation.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Political revolt is a *decisional volitional act* — it is not a gradual drift or a habitual condition but a specific decisive action. 2Ki 8:20 — "Edom revolted from the rule of Judah and set up a king of their own" — the revolt is an event with a specific act (setting up their own king). 2Ki 1:1 — "After the death of Ahab, Moab rebelled" — the revolt has a triggering moment (Ahab's death). The characteristic is act-like rather than dispositional: it is the specific moment of breaking submission rather than the ongoing habitual pattern of non-compliance (which would be CHAR-5/6 territory). The 9-verse corpus focuses entirely on the act and its persistent consequences, not on the ongoing disposition of the revolting party.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The corpus reveals sinful speech as operating across three distinct kinds. (1) *An act with an inner origin*: VCG-01's slander is an act (the speaking of harmful words) that originates in the heart's corruption (Mat 15:19). It is both an inner condition (corrupted heart) and an outer act (the speech). (2) *A presumptuous claim*: VCG-02's blasphemy-as-divine-authority-claim is an act of presumption — claiming what belongs to God alone (Mar 2:7; Joh 10:33). (3) *A constitutive identity*: VCG-03's beast-blasphemy is not primarily an act but a defining inner character — "blasphemous names on its heads" (Rev 13:1), "full of blasphemous names" (Rev 17:3) — the creature's very being is constituted by its defiance of God. The characteristic spans act, presumption, and constituted character.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Three distinct kinds across the mechanisms. (1) *Enticement*: a *process* — the act of being drawn through one's own desire toward sin (Jam 1:14). The characteristic in this mechanism is not a single discrete act but a process of being lured through the desire-faculty. (2) *Hypocrisy*: a *social-identity act* — the adoption of a performed identity alongside others (Gal 2:13 — "acted hypocritically along with him"). This is primarily an act with an identity-distorting consequence. (3) *Apostasy*: a *condition/event* — the state of having departed from covenantal standing (Act 21:21 — the accusation of "forsaking Moses"). The apostasia may be a specific event that produces an ongoing condition.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The corpus establishes CHAR-11 as a *structural condition* — a state of being — rather than an act, disposition, or quality in isolation. The three VCG registers name it differently: (1) VCG-01 — a *defining inner quality* (rish.ah — wickedness as the person's settled moral character: Mal 1:4 — "the wicked country"); (2) VCG-02 — a *standing moral debt* before God (requiring forgiveness — Mat 9:2; requiring blotting out — Act 3:19; requiring covering — Rom 4:7); (3) VCG-03 — an *enslaving power* (hamartia as a ruling master: Rom 5:21 — "sin reigned"; Rom 6:23 — "sin pays wages"; Rom 7:17 — "sin that dwells within me"). The characteristic thus operates simultaneously as quality (VCG-01), as debt (VCG-02), and as power (VCG-03) — three faces of the same underlying structural condition.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The corpus reveals a *power-dynamic condition* — sin operating as an active governing force over the inner person. Joh 8:34 — "a slave to sin" — the slave is in a condition, not performing an act; the slavery is a constitutional state produced by practice. Gal 3:22 — "imprisoned everything under sin" — the imprisonment is a structural condition, not a moment-by-moment act. 1Jo 3:8 — "of the devil" — the enslaving power reaches the level of constituted allegiance: the person's deepest identity-marker is shaped by the master to whom they are enslaved. The characteristic is thus primarily a *dispositional power-condition* — the state of being under sin's governing authority — rather than a specific act or momentary event.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-13 as a *standing objective moral condition* — not primarily an act (CHAR-1) or a feeling (CHAR-17) but the accumulated moral weight that sits before God as a fact. Hos 13:12 — "his sin is kept in store" — the record is a stored reality; Exo 32:34 — "in the day when I visit, I will visit their sin upon them" — the record is a deferred moral account. The characteristic operates at the objective rather than subjective level: the record exists whether the person feels its weight or not (definition: "the person carries this record whether they feel it or not"). It is thus a *condition of standing* before God rather than an inner experience.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The seeking dimension is a *volitional-relational act*: the will's turn toward God in appeal. Exo 32:30 — "I will go up to the Lord; perhaps I can make atonement for your sin" — Moses's going up is a volitional movement; Act 2:38 — "repent and be baptized" — the repentance is an inner volitional turn. The receiving dimension is a *relational-condition change*: the person's standing before God is altered by the divine declaration. Luk 7:48 — "your sins are forgiven" — the declaration changes the woman's relational condition before God. Col 2:13 — "dead in your trespasses... made alive" — the received forgiveness is a constitutional condition change. The characteristic is thus a *volitional act (seeking) producing a relational-condition change (receiving)*.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The corpus reveals a *corporate moral condition sustained across generations* — not primarily a discrete act but an embedded sinning-pattern that communities carry. Psa 78:17/32 — the wilderness generation sinned persistently in a pattern; Isa 64:5 — "in our sins we have been a long time" — the prolonged state confirms this is a condition, not a momentary act. However, the corpus also reveals individual participation in the corporate condition: 1Ki 8:47 — "we have sinned" — each member of the exiled community confesses within the corporate "we." The characteristic is thus a *corporate-condition in which individuals participate* rather than a purely individual condition.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The corpus reveals a *constituted moral identity* — the person not merely performing sinful acts but having become a recognisable moral type. The three VCG registers name it differently: (1) VCG-03 — extreme habitual wrongdoers (Gen 13:13 — Sodom; 1Sa 15:18 — Amalekites) — the sinner-identity as totality of moral corruption; (2) VCG-01 — social-moral category carrying exclusion and stigma (Psa 1:5 — excluded from congregation; Mat 9:10 — social category at table) — the sinner-identity as publicly recognised status; (3) VCG-02 — constituted/imputed status (Rom 5:19 — made sinners through Adam) — the sinner-identity as formally constituted moral standing. Across all three registers: a recognisable moral category, not merely a description of acts.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-17 as a *sustained inner condition of the conscience engaged in moral-liability awareness*. The definition names it: "the conscience's recognition of moral liability before God — the subjective inner experience of being guilty." Three distinct inner registers within the condition: (1) *Guilt-recognition* (VCG-01): the moment of conscience-awareness arising when a violation "becomes known" — Lev 5:5 — "when he realizes his guilt"; (2) *Guilt-as-accumulated-weight* (VCG-02): the sustained condition of carrying moral liability — Ezr 9:6 — guilt mounted to the heavens; (3) *Guilt-as-consciousness-of-punitive-liability* (VCG-04 — a.von H5771I): Gen 4:13 — Cain's felt unbearability of the punishment-dimension of his guilt.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-18 as an *objective moral-legal condition* before God — not primarily a subjective feeling (that is CHAR-17) but the actual crime-weight the person carries in God's moral ledger. The definition identifies it as "the inner dimension of accumulated moral wrongdoing — crime in its moral-spiritual sense." The characteristic's five dominant registers illuminate its nature: (1) personal moral crime individually owned (VCG-04 — Eze 18:20 — "upon himself"); (2) accumulated weight that overwhelms (VCG-05 — Psa 38:4 — "gone over my head"); (3) generational crime transmitted across family lines (VCG-02 — Exo 20:5); (4) iniquity in divine memory (VCG-03 — Hos 13:12 — "bound up"); (5) iniquity that ruins from within (VCG-07 — Pro 5:22 — "ensnare him").

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-19 as a *volitional act-and-disposition complex*: primarily a wilful act of the will (the specific crossing) but also a dispositional orientation of the will that has become constitutive. (1) *Act dimension*: Amos 1:3/6/9 — specific counted acts that cross the threshold; Rom 4:15 — the law-violation as a specific act; Eze 18:28 — "all the transgressions that he had committed" — enumerable past acts; (2) *Dispositional dimension*: Psa 36:1 — "transgression speaks to the wicked deep in his heart" — an inner orientation that has become a voice; Isa 57:4 — "children of transgression" — transgression as identity; Isa 48:8 — "from before birth called a rebel" — a constitutive inner character.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-20 as primarily a *dispositional inner character that expresses through volitional acts of betrayal*. The three VCGs illuminate this: (1) VCG-01 — unfaithfulness toward God: Eze 14:13 — "a land sins against me by acting faithlessly" — faithlessness as the quality of a land's settled sin-posture; (2) VCG-02 — interpersonal treachery: Pro 11:3 — "the crookedness of the treacherous destroys them" — treachery as a crookedness of character; (3) VCG-03 — constitutional character: Isa 24:16 — "the traitors have betrayed, with betrayal the traitors have betrayed" — the person IS the betrayal; Isa 48:8 — foreknown constitutional bent. The characteristic is more disposition than discrete act — the will's settled orientation of betrayal.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-21 as a *multi-faculty inner distortion condition* that operates as both a disposition and a process. (1) *Dispositional dimension*: Deu 32:20 — "a perverse generation" — perversity as a generational character trait; Pro 12:8 — "twisted mind" — perversity as a constitutive feature of the mind; 1Ti 6:5 — "depraved in mind" — the depravity as settled condition; (2) *Process dimension*: Eph 4:22 — "is corrupt through deceitful desires" — active progressive corruption through desire; 2Pe 2:19 — "slaves of corruption" — the progressive capture; Jer 2:21 — "turned degenerate and become a wild vine" — a transformation-process of moral decay.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The corpus reveals CHAR-22 as simultaneously a *dispositional character trait* and a *volitional act*. (1) *Dispositional*: Luk 16:10 — "dishonest in a very little is also dishonest in much" — a fixed inner character property that scales; Job 15:16 — "drinks injustice like water" — a thoroughly internalized disposition; Zep 3:5 — "the unjust knows no shame" — shamelessness as a stable character-trait; (2) *Volitional act*: Psa 58:2 — "in your hearts you devise wrongs; your hands deal out violence" — a deliberate inner devising; Eze 18:24 — "a righteous person... does injustice" — a specific act committed; Job 34:32 — "if I have done iniquity, I will do it no more" — injustice as a named doable-and-stoppable act.

---

### T1.2.2

**Question:** Is this characteristic simple in structure or does it appear to have constituent elements at first encounter?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — At first encounter the characteristic has constituent elements.
